# 2002\n
{Oxford, Bodleian Library, Digby 86, all the texts that are suitable for mapping}\n
C13b2\n
375 232 N\n
{=NW Gloucs=}\n
{=Sample tagged - the texts on fols. 119r-143r, 163v-164r, 165r-168v,\n
195v-200r, 206r. This sample consists of all the verse texts in Middle\n
English in the MS, but excluding, XI Pains of Hell, Proverbs of\n
Hending, Dame Sirith and Debate between the Body and Soul, which all\n
show signs of linguistic mixture=}\n
{=Script - Anglicana. For a full description see Tschann and Parkes\n
(1996:xxxviii-xli)=}\n
{=Status - MS punctuation done; tagging notes and textual notes up to date=}\n
{=Punctuation is mainly confined to punctus at line ends and then\n
usually only to define the end of the line in column a when it is\n
long enough to threaten to encroach on column b. When a line is too\n
long to be accommodated in its own space, the scribe takes the\n
overflow to the end of a neighbouring line that is shorter and\n
separates it from the text of that line by a paraph. The end of this\n
shorter line is often separated from the paraph by a punctus. The\n
insertion point and the overflow text are marked with a double hyphen.\n
Capital *I in whatever context has a punctus on either side of it. This\n
is taken to be part of the capitalisation of the letter and is not\n
separately noted. Note also *W. W has its origins in two ligatured Vs.\n
Anglicana W begins like this, but with increasing curvature of the\n
second stroke of V, a quicker way of forming it is often used, and the\n
Digby scribe adopts a version of this. His W is formed by two slanting\n
parallel strokes with a 3-shaped tailed third element abutting the\n
second oblique stroke. When a capital is intended - most often\n
at the beginning of a verse line - the shape is identical but is filled\n
with colour to form a littera notabilior. This is what I mark as *W.\n
However, the Digby scribe seems never to colour single V in this way.\n
So some examples of *W may be functioning as capital *V, in the same\n
way as initial double F functions as capital *F. In the case of F,\n
initial doubling cannot imply anything else than a capitalisation of the\n
simplex and it is a common convention in medieval scripts. Digby\'s use\n
of *W is ambiguous and idiosyncratic and to differentiate *W and *V by\n
context would obscure the fact that a single symbol is used for both.\n
Note too that the Digby scribe does occasionally use ordinary W in\n
[v]/[u]  contexts as well as V/U in [w] contexts=}\n

$/P11N_ICH\n
!_MIzTEN\n
!_HEUEED\n
{=CB13 divides as ICH MIzT EN HEUEED, presumably "While I might on\n
head". But I cannot make sense of this and I assume there is textual\n
corruption. This passage in Harley 2253\'s version is entirely about\n
Maximian\'s wife and the pronoun is consistently HUE. Here there is\n
clearly a somewhat different text because Maximian\'s friends are\n
introduced. I suspect that HI or HY in the exemplar for "they" has\n
been taken to be for the similarly spelled first person singular which\n
the Digby scribe here translates to his preferred spelling ICH. This\n
would explain the form MIzTEN which would then be correctly plural.\n
HEUEED may be a rationalisation to the word for "head" from exemplar\n
HEED or HEEDE. The whole might have been: "they consider me to be a bad\n
lot, while they might take heed"=}\n
{\\}\n
$beat/vSpp_*I-BET+EN $ge-/xp-vpp_*I- $/vSpp_+EN\n
$havenot/vpt11_N+E+DDE $n-/xp-neg_N+ $/vpt13[V]_+DDE\n
$/P11N+H_ICH\n
!_HOE\n
{=This is not plausible as a form for the accusative of the third person\n
singular of the feminine personal pronoun nor of the plural. We therefore\n
have two subjects and the textual corruption seems to continue. I\n
suggest the original would have had "had they [or she] not beaten me"\n
with ICH being erroneously substituted for ME. Or, syntactically more\n
probable, "if I had not been beaten", with HOE being written for BE\n
(via BOE?) which fits the rhyme-scheme and would be a form of the past\n
participle of the verb "be"=} \n
{\\}\n
$christ/n-voc_*CRIST\n

WROwE from OE *wra:h or *wra:g to make pure rhyme with earlier LOWE.\n

early short <a>. See MED s.v. wrau adj so this may in fact have been\n
a half-rhyme in the original. There are several other examples of half\n
rhymes in this verse, e.g OUERCOME/SONE, WRONG/LOND, BERN/BEDLEHEM=}\n

$westlooker/n{rh}_WEST-LOK+ERE $looker/n-k{rh}_-LOK+ERE $-er/xs-agt-n{rh}_+ERE\n
{=This is better than "stye-looker" - "the bleary-eyed one" as suggested\n
by Ross (1935) and it is backed up by Medulla Grammatice entries for\n
west-lokere i.e. \'a lokere west\' etc glossing petus (Classical Latin\n
paetus. The meaning seems to be "divergent squinter", "away-looker" i.e.\n
"one that has its eyes on the side of the head". Cf. SID-LOKERE,\n
BRODLOKERE and WALDENEIE. See further Laing (1998) - Medium Aevum=} \n

$roughlooker?/n_ROU+LOK+ERE $looker?/n-k_+LOK+ERE $-er/xs-agt-n_+ERE\n
{=This is taking what has been read LEKERE to read LOKERE which I believe\n
it plausibly does. "Rough-looker" I take to mean "fierce-looker" or\n
"glarer" - see Laing (1998) - Medium Aevum. Ross (1935) reads +LEKERE\n
and suggests "frisker" from OScand leika OE la:can, this is not\n
otherwise recorded before C19. However the verb leiken is fine at this\n
date and the formation seems possible though unusual in not having -EIK-\n
or -AIK-. Cf Digby 86 Debate of Body and Soul LEIKEN vi. MED, for some\n
reason, seems to accept "frisker" for the form here, but to think\n
LEKERE below is a form of the word for "lecher". I assume that one\n
ought to take the two LEKEREs as being the same word not different\n
ones if one reads LEKERE for both. The rhyme here, however, supports\n
the reading LOKERE rather than LEKERE though cf. LEKERE below. Ross\n
may be right but ROU is also difficult as a form from OE rae:w. Digby\n
does however have ROWE in rhyme in the Thrush and the Nightingale. If\n
LOKERE is accepted however, "rough", meaning "fierce" from OE ru:h, ru:g,\n
ru:we is the best reading - see MED s.v. rough(e adj and OED s.v.row\n
a1 and row adv}. Raw-looker is perhaps equally possible, from OE\n
hre:aw/hre:ow. See MED raue aj. NB. "raw-eyes" implies prominent eyes\n
and OED row a2, cf. raw-eyed. See further also Laing (1998)=}\n
{\\}\n

$cower-on-hill/n{rh}_COURE-HO+HULLE $on{p}/pr_-HO+ $hill/n<pr-k{rh}_+HULLE\n
{=See Laing (1998). MS appears to have TOURE HOHULLE. Ross (1935) emends to\n
TOURE-TOHULLE and translates "the one who directs one\'s steps to the\n
hills". I don\'t think this plausible or a justifiable emendation.\n
Digby has at least three examples of HON for "on", while HO for "on"\n
is found once each in Clerico et Puella and in the Egerton 613 Somer\n
is comen verse. What you do on a hill if you\'re a hare is not "tour"\n
"tower" or "turn" but "cower" in the sense "lie low, skulk". Ross\n
points out that hares can outrun dogs uphill because of their leg\n
shape, but once on the hill they must skulk because down-hill they\n
are at a considerable disadvantage and once at the summit they will\n
be caught. See G.E. Evans and D. Thomson, The Leaping Hare, London\n
1972, p. 33. This phrase does not appear in any form in  MED or OED,\n
apparently. For examples, which can be added to, of T-shape for C in\n
Digby see CON 119ra l 14, COME 119vb l 28, COMEy 120ra l 10, CUNNE\n
120rb line 19, COME 120va line 22 etc. and cf. COUARD for "toward"\n
fol. 121vb line 33=}\n

{=Between this text and the previous are seven texts in French and then\n
the Debate between the Body and the Soul (fols. 195v-197v) in mixed\n
language not included here. It is tagged separately as text no. 222.\n

separate coloured title. The scribe seems to have treated this, the\n
previous text and the next text (no 224) as a single text. The four lines of\n
each rhyming quatrain are joined by wiggly braces in the right margin=} \n

{=No space break between this text and the previous and no coloured\n
initial introducing it. Whether or not the texts were separate in his\n
exemplar, and in spite of the intervening \'amen\', the scribe here treats\n
the two as a single text, possibly also as a unit with \'The Debate\n
between the Body and the Soul\' fols 195v-197v, not included in this\n
sample because it is in mixed language, but tagged separately as text\n
no. 222. As with the previous two texts, the four lines of each\n
rhyming quatrain are joined by wiggly braces in the right margin=}\n
{~f198r}\n
